<p>Augustus (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> 27 BC – AD 14</span>) gathered almost all the republican powers under his official title, <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">princeps</a></i>: he had the powers of consul, <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps_senatus" title="Princeps senatus">princeps senatus</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Aedile" title="Aedile">aedile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_censor" title="Roman censor">censor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tribune" title="Tribune">tribune</a>—including tribunician sacrosanctity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> This was the base of an emperor's power. Augustus also styled himself as <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">Imperator</a> Gaius Julius Caesar divi filius</i>, "Commander Gaius Julius Caesar, son of the deified one". With this title he not only boasted his familial link to deified Julius Caesar, but the use of <i>Imperator</i> signified a permanent link to the Roman tradition of victory.
</p><p>He also diminished the political influence of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Senate" title="Roman Senate">senatorial class</a> by boosting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_class" title="Equestrian class">equestrian class</a>. The senators lost their right to rule certain provinces, like Egypt, since the governor of that province was directly nominated by the emperor. The creation of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_Guard" title="Praetorian Guard">Praetorian Guard</a> and his reforms in the military, creating a <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> with a fixed size of 28 legions, ensured his total control over the army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> Compared with the Second Triumvirate's epoch, Augustus' reign as <i>princeps</i> was very peaceful. This peace and wealth (obtained from the agrarian province of Egypt)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> led the people and the nobles of Rome to support Augustus, increasing his strength in political affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> Augustus was absent at battles. His generals were responsible for the field command; gaining such commanders as <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus">Nero Claudius Drusus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a> much respect from the populace and the legions. Augustus intended to extend the Roman Empire to the whole known world, and in his reign, Rome conquered <a href="/wiki/Cantabrian_Wars" title="Cantabrian Wars">Cantabria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aquitania" title="Aquitania">Aquitania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raetia" title="Raetia">Raetia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dalmatia" title="Dalmatia">Dalmatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pannonia" title="Pannonia">Pannonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under Augustus' reign, Roman literature grew steadily in what is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_Latin_Literature" title="Golden Age of Latin Literature">Golden Age of Latin Literature</a>. Poets like <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horace" title="Horace">Horace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Varius_Rufus" title="Lucius Varius Rufus">Rufus</a> developed a rich literature, and were close friends of Augustus. Along with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maecenas" title="Maecenas">Maecenas</a>, he sponsored patriotic poems, as Virgil's epic <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i> and also historiographical works, like those of <a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a>. The works of this literary age lasted through Roman times, and are classics. Augustus also continued the changes to the calendar promoted by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar</a>, and the month of August is named after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup> Augustus brought a peaceful and thriving era to Rome, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Romana" title="Pax Romana">Pax Augusta</a></i> or <i>Pax Romana</i>. Augustus died in 14 AD, but the empire's glory continued after his era.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Tiberius_to_Nero">From Tiberius to Nero</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: From Tiberius to Nero">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/300px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/450px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg/600px-Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Impero_romano_sotto_Ottaviano_Augusto_30aC_-_6dC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extent of the Roman Empire under Augustus. The yellow legend represents the extent of the Republic in 31 BC, the shades of green represent gradually conquered territories under the reign of Augustus, and pink areas on the map represent <a href="/wiki/Client_state" title="Client state">client states</a>; areas under Roman control shown here were subject to change even during Augustus' reign, especially in <a href="/wiki/Germania" title="Germania">Germania</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Julio-Claudians continued to rule Rome after Augustus' death and remained in power until the death of Nero in 68 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup> Augustus' favorites to succeed him were already dead in his senescence: his nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Claudius_Marcellus_(Julio-Claudian_dynasty)" title="Marcus Claudius Marcellus (Julio-Claudian dynasty)">Marcellus</a> died in 23 BC, his friend and military commander <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Vipsanius_Agrippa" title="Marcus Vipsanius Agrippa">Agrippa</a> in 12 BC and his grandson <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Caesar" title="Gaius Caesar">Gaius Caesar</a> in 4 AD. Influenced by his wife, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livia_Drusilla" title="Livia Drusilla">Livia Drusilla</a>, Augustus appointed her son from another marriage, <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a>, as his heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Senate agreed with the succession, and granted to Tiberius the same titles and honors once granted to Augustus: the title of <i>princeps</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pater_patriae" title="Pater patriae">Pater patriae</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Civic_Crown" title="Civic Crown">Civic Crown</a>. However, Tiberius was not an enthusiast for political affairs: after agreement with the Senate, he retired to <a href="/wiki/Capri" title="Capri">Capri</a> in 26 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> and left control of the city of Rome in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">praetorian prefect</a> <a href="/wiki/Sejanus" title="Sejanus">Sejanus</a> (until 31 AD) and <a href="/wiki/Naevius_Sutorius_Macro" title="Naevius Sutorius Macro">Macro</a> (from 31 to 37 AD). Tiberius was regarded as an evil and melancholic man, who may have ordered the murder of his relatives, the popular general <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a> in 19 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tarver1902_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-tarver1902-81">[79]</a></sup> and his own son <a href="/wiki/Drusus_Julius_Caesar" title="Drusus Julius Caesar">Drusus Julius Caesar</a> in 23 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tarver1902_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-tarver1902-81">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tiberius died (or was killed)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tarver1902_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-tarver1902-81">[79]</a></sup> in 37 AD. The male line of the Julio-Claudians was limited to Tiberius' nephew <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a>, his grandson <a href="/wiki/Tiberius_Gemellus" title="Tiberius Gemellus">Tiberius Gemellus</a> and his grand-nephew <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a>. As Gemellus was still a child, Caligula was chosen to rule the empire. He was a popular leader in the first half of his reign, but became a crude and insane tyrant in his years controlling government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> states that he committed <a href="/wiki/Incest" title="Incest">incest</a> with his sisters, killed some men just for amusement and nominated <a href="/wiki/Incitatus" title="Incitatus">a horse</a> for a consulship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup> The Praetorian Guard murdered Caligula four years after the death of Tiberius,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup> and, with belated support from the senators, proclaimed his uncle <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> as the new emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Claudius was not as authoritarian as Tiberius and Caligula. Claudius conquered <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Thrace" title="Roman Thrace">Thrace</a>; his most important deed was the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">conquest of Britannia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> Claudius was poisoned by his wife, <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Younger" title="Agrippina the Younger">Agrippina the Younger</a> in 54 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> His heir was <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>, son of Agrippina and her former husband, since Claudius' son <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a> had not reached manhood upon his father's death.
</p><p>Nero sent his general, <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Suetonius_Paulinus" title="Gaius Suetonius Paulinus">Suetonius Paulinus</a>, to invade modern-day <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, where he encountered stiff resistance. The <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Britons" title="Celtic Britons">Celts</a> there were independent, tough and resistant to tax collectors and fought Paulinus, as he battled his way across from east to west. It took him a long time to reach the north west coast, and in 60 AD he finally crossed the <a href="/wiki/Menai_Strait" title="Menai Strait">Menai Strait</a> to the sacred island of Mona (<a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a>), the last stronghold of the <a href="/wiki/Druid" title="Druid">druids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup> His soldiers <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Anglesey" title="Roman conquest of Anglesey">attacked the island</a> and massacred the druids: men, women and children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> destroyed the shrine and the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_grove" title="Sacred grove">sacred groves</a> and threw many of the sacred standing stones into the sea. While Paulinus and his troops were massacring druids in Mona, the tribes of modern-day <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> staged a revolt led by queen <a href="/wiki/Boudica" title="Boudica">Boadicea</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Iceni" title="Iceni">Iceni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup> The rebels sacked and burned <a href="/wiki/Camulodunum" title="Camulodunum">Camulodunum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Londinium" title="Londinium">Londinium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verulamium" title="Verulamium">Verulamium</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Colchester" title="Colchester">Colchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and <a href="/wiki/St_Albans" title="St Albans">St Albans</a> respectively) before they were <a href="/wiki/Defeat_of_Boudica" title="Defeat of Boudica">crushed by Paulinus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> Boadicea, like <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra" title="Cleopatra">Cleopatra</a> before her, committed suicide to avoid the disgrace of being paraded in triumph in Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup> The fault of Nero in this rebellion is debatable but there was certainly an impact (both positive and negative) upon the prestige of his regime.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Nero is widely known as the first persecutor of Christians and for the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a>, rumoured to have been started by the emperor himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> In 59 AD he murdered his mother and in 62 AD, his wife <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Octavia" title="Claudia Octavia">Claudia Octavia</a>. Never very stable, he allowed his advisers to run the government while he slid into debauchery, excess, and madness. He was married three times, and had numerous affairs with both men and women, and, according to some rumors, even his mother. A conspiracy against Nero in 65 AD under <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Calpurnius_Piso_(conspirator)" title="Gaius Calpurnius Piso (conspirator)">Calpurnius Piso</a> failed, but in 68 AD the armies under <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Julius_Vindex" title="Gaius Julius Vindex">Julius Vindex</a> in Gaul and <a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Servius Sulpicius Galba</a> in modern-day Spain revolted. Deserted by the Praetorian Guards and condemned to death by the senate, Nero killed himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flavian_dynasty">Flavian dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Flavian dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="759" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png/170px-Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png/255px-Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png/340px-Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vespasianus01_pushkin_edit.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Vespasian</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavian dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">Flavians</a> were the second dynasty to rule Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suetonius_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-suetonius-95">[93]</a></sup> By 68 AD, the year of Nero's death, there was no chance of a return to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, and so a new emperor had to arise. After the turmoil in the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vespasian" title="Vespasian">Titus Flavius Vespasianus</a> (anglicized as Vespasian) took control of the empire and established a new dynasty. Under the Flavians, Rome continued its expansion, and the state remained secure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most significant <a href="/wiki/Campaign_history_of_the_Roman_military" title="Campaign history of the Roman military">military campaign</a> undertaken during the Flavian period was the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(70_CE)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (70 CE)">siege and destruction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> in 70 by <a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">Titus</a>. The destruction of the city was the culmination of the Roman campaign in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judea_(Roman_province)" title="Judea (Roman province)">Judea</a> following the Jewish uprising of 66. The Second Temple was completely demolished, after which Titus' soldiers proclaimed him <i><a href="/wiki/Imperator" title="Imperator">imperator</a></i> in honor of the victory. Jerusalem was sacked and much of the population killed or dispersed. <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> claims that 1,100,000 people were killed during the siege, of whom a majority were Jewish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[95]</a></sup> 97,000 were captured and <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_ancient_Rome" title="Slavery in ancient Rome">enslaved</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Simon_bar_Giora" title="Simon bar Giora">Simon bar Giora</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_of_Giscala" title="John of Giscala">John of Giscala</a>. Many fled to areas around the Mediterranean. Titus reportedly refused to accept a wreath of victory, as there was "no merit in vanquishing people forsaken by their own God".
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vespasian">Vespasian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Vespasian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Vespasian had been a general under <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a>. He fought as a commander in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_Jewish-Roman_War" title="First Jewish-Roman War">First Jewish-Roman War</a> along with his son <a href="/wiki/Titus" title="Titus">Titus</a>. Following the turmoil of the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Four_Emperors" title="Year of the Four Emperors">Year of the Four Emperors</a>, in 69 AD, four emperors were enthroned in turn: <a href="/wiki/Galba" title="Galba">Galba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otho" title="Otho">Otho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitellius" title="Vitellius">Vitellius</a>, and, lastly, Vespasian, who crushed Vitellius' forces and became emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suetonius_Vespasian_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suetonius_Vespasian-98">[96]</a></sup> He reconstructed many buildings which were uncompleted, like a statue of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> and the temple of <i><a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Divus Claudius</a></i> ("the deified Claudius"), both initiated by Nero. Buildings destroyed by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_Rome" title="Great Fire of Rome">Great Fire of Rome</a> were rebuilt, and he revitalized the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Hill" title="Capitoline Hill">Capitol</a>. Vespasian also started the construction of the Flavian Amphitheater, more commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suetonius_Vespasian_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-Suetonius_Vespasian-98">[96]</a></sup> The historians <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> wrote their works during Vespasian's reign. Vespasian was Josephus' sponsor and Pliny dedicated his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">Naturalis Historia</a></i> to Titus, son of Vespasian. Vespasian sent legions to defend the eastern frontier in <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, extended the occupation in Britannia (modern-day <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, Wales and southern <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>) and reformed the tax system. He died in 79 AD.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Titus_and_Domitian">Titus and Domitian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Titus and Domitian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Titus had a short-lived rule; he was emperor from 79 to 81 AD. He finished the Flavian Amphitheater, which was constructed with war spoils from the First Jewish-Roman War, and promoted games celebrating the victory over the Jews that lasted for a hundred days. These games included <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiatorial combats</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chariot_race" title="Chariot race">chariot races</a> and a sensational mock naval battle on the flooded grounds of the Colosseum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[97]</a></sup> Titus died of fever in 81 AD, and was succeeded by his brother <a href="/wiki/Domitian" title="Domitian">Domitian</a>. As emperor, Domitian assumed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Totalitarian" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> characteristics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup> thought he could be a new Augustus, and tried to make a personal cult of himself. Domitian ruled for fifteen years, and his reign was marked by his attempts to compare himself to the gods. He constructed at least two temples in honour of Jupiter, the supreme deity in <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman religion</a>. He also liked to be called "<i>Dominus et Deus</i>" ("Master and God").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Nerva.E2.80.93Antonine_dynasty"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nerva–Antonine_dynasty">Nerva–Antonine dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nerva–Antonine dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="489" data-file-width="791" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png/220px-RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png/330px-RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png/440px-RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RomanEmpireTrajan117AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> reached its greatest extent under <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> in AD 117</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nerva%E2%80%93Antonine_dynasty" title="Nerva–Antonine dynasty">Nerva–Antonine dynasty</a> from 96 AD to 192 AD included the reigns of the emperors <a href="/wiki/Nerva" title="Nerva">Nerva</a>, Trajan, Hadrian, Antoninus Pius, Marcus Aurelius, Lucius Verus, and Commodus. During this time, Rome reached its greatest territorial and economic extent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup> This was a time of peace for Rome. The criteria for choosing an emperor were the qualities of the candidate and no longer ties of kinship; additionally, there were no civil wars or military defeats in this period. Following Domitian's murder, the Senate rapidly appointed Nerva to hold the imperial dignity. This was the first time that senators chose the emperor since Octavian had been honored with the titles of <i><a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">princeps</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_(honorific)" title="Augustus (honorific)">Augustus</a></i>. Nerva had a noble ancestry, and he had served as an advisor to Nero and the Flavians. His rule restored many of the liberties once assumed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> by Domitian<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> and started the last golden era of Rome.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trajan">Trajan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Trajan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traiani_iustitia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="789" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Traiani_iustitia.jpg/220px-Traiani_iustitia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Traiani_iustitia.jpg/330px-Traiani_iustitia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Traiani_iustitia.jpg/440px-Traiani_iustitia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traiani_iustitia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Justice of Trajan</i> (fragment) by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix" title="Eugène Delacroix">Eugène Delacroix</a></div></div></div>
<p>Nerva died in 98 AD and his successor and heir was the general <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a>. Trajan was born in a non-patrician family from <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Baetica" title="Hispania Baetica">Hispania Baetica</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Andalusia" title="Andalusia">Andalusia</a>) and his preeminence emerged in the army, under Domitian. He is the second of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Good_Emperors" title="Five Good Emperors">Five Good Emperors</a>, the first being Nerva. Trajan was greeted by the people of Rome with enthusiasm, which he justified by governing well and without the bloodiness that had marked Domitian's reign. He freed many people who had been unjustly imprisoned by Domitian and returned private property that Domitian had confiscated; a process begun by Nerva before his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Dacian_Wars" title="Trajan's Dacian Wars">Trajan conquered Dacia</a> (roughly modern-day <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a>), and defeated the king <a href="/wiki/Decebalus" title="Decebalus">Decebalus</a>, who had <a href="/wiki/Domitian%27s_Dacian_War" title="Domitian's Dacian War">defeated Domitian's forces</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/First_Dacian_War" title="First Dacian War">First Dacian War</a> (101–102), the defeated <a href="/wiki/Dacia" title="Dacia">Dacia</a> became a client kingdom; in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Dacian_War" title="Second Dacian War">Second Dacian War</a> (105–106), Trajan completely devastated the enemy's resistance and annexed Dacia to the Empire. Trajan also annexed the client state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabatea" title="Nabatea">Nabatea</a> to form the province of <a href="/wiki/Arabia_Petraea" title="Arabia Petraea">Arabia Petraea</a>, which included the lands of southern Syria and northwestern Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup> He erected many buildings that survive to this day, such as <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Forum" title="Trajan's Forum">Trajan's Forum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Market" title="Trajan's Market">Trajan's Market</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Column" title="Trajan's Column">Trajan's Column</a>. His main architect was <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_of_Damascus" title="Apollodorus of Damascus">Apollodorus of Damascus</a>; Apollodorus made the project of the Forum and of the Column, and also reformed the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a>. Trajan's triumphal arches in <a href="/wiki/Ancona" title="Ancona">Ancona</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benevento" title="Benevento">Beneventum</a> are other constructions projected by him. In the Second Dacian War, Apollodorus made a <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Bridge" title="Trajan's Bridge">great bridge over the Danube</a> for Trajan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Parthian_campaign" title="Trajan's Parthian campaign">Trajan's final war</a> was against <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>. When Parthia appointed a king for <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenia</a> who was unacceptable to Rome (Parthia and Rome shared dominance over Armenia), he declared war. He probably wanted to be the first Roman leader to conquer Parthia, and repeat the glory of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, conqueror of Asia, whom Trajan next followed in the clash of Greek-Romans and the Persian cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> In 113 he marched to Armenia and deposed the local king. In 115 Trajan turned south into the core of Parthian hegemony, took the Northern Mesopotamian cities of <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">Nisibis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suru%C3%A7" title="Suruç">Batnae</a>, organized a province of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia_(Roman_province)" title="Mesopotamia (Roman province)">Mesopotamia</a> (116), and issued coins announcing that Armenia and Mesopotamia were under the authority of the Roman people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> In that same year, he captured <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> and the Parthian capital <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> (near modern <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> After defeating a Parthian revolt and a <a href="/wiki/Kitos_War" title="Kitos War">Jewish revolt</a>, he withdrew due to health issues. In 117, his illness grew and he died of <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">edema</a>. He nominated <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a> as his heir. Under Trajan's leadership the Roman Empire reached the peak of its territorial expansion;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlas_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-110">[108]</a></sup> Rome's dominion now spanned 5.0 million square kilometres (1.9 million square miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Hadrian_to_Commodus">From Hadrian to Commodus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: From Hadrian to Commodus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hadrians_Wall_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="995" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hadrians_Wall_map.png/220px-Hadrians_Wall_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hadrians_Wall_map.png/330px-Hadrians_Wall_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Hadrians_Wall_map.png/440px-Hadrians_Wall_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hadrians_Wall_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the location of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a> in Scotland and Northern England</div></div></div>
<p>Many Romans emigrated to Hispania (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>) and stayed for generations, in some cases intermarrying with <a href="/wiki/Iberians" title="Iberians">Iberians</a>; one of these families produced the emperor <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> Hadrian withdrew all the troops stationed in Parthia, Armenia and Mesopotamia (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), abandoning Trajan's conquests. Hadrian's army crushed a revolt in <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bar_Kokhba_revolt" title="Bar Kokhba revolt">Bar Kokhba revolt</a> in Judea. This was the last large-scale Jewish revolt against the Romans, and was suppressed with massive repercussions in Judea. Hundreds of thousands of Jews were killed. Hadrian renamed the province of Judea "<a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Provincia Syria Palaestina</a>," after one of Judea's most hated enemies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup> He constructed fortifications and walls, like the celebrated <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> which separated Roman Britannia and the tribes of modern-day Scotland. Hadrian promoted culture, especially the Greek. He also forbade <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> and humanized the laws. His many building projects included aqueducts, baths, libraries and theaters; additionally, he travelled nearly every province in the Empire to check the military and infrastructural conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup> Following Hadrian's death in 138 AD, his successor <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> built temples, theaters, and mausoleums, promoted the arts and sciences, and bestowed honours and financial rewards upon the teachers of <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> and philosophy. On becoming emperor, Antoninus made few initial changes, leaving intact as far as possible the arrangements instituted by his predecessor. Antoninus expanded Roman Britannia by invading what is now southern Scotland and building the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Wall" title="Antonine Wall">Antonine Wall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> He also continued Hadrian's policy of humanizing the laws. He died in 161 AD.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg/220px-20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg/330px-20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg/440px-20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:20190406-DSC5193_Panteon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon, Rome</a>, built during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian" title="Hadrian">Hadrian</a>, which still contains the largest unreinforced <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a> in the world</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a>, known as the Philosopher, was the last of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Good_Emperors" title="Five Good Emperors">Five Good Emperors</a>. He was a stoic philosopher and wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Meditations" title="Meditations">Meditations</a></i>. He defeated barbarian tribes in the <a href="/wiki/Marcomannic_Wars" title="Marcomannic Wars">Marcomannic Wars</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup> His co-emperor, <a href="/wiki/Lucius_Verus" title="Lucius Verus">Lucius Verus</a>, died in 169 AD, probably from the <a href="/wiki/Antonine_Plague" title="Antonine Plague">Antonine Plague</a>, a pandemic that killed nearly five million people through the Empire in 165–180 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Nerva to Marcus Aurelius, the empire achieved an unprecedented status. The powerful influence of laws and manners had gradually cemented the union of the provinces. All the citizens enjoyed and abused the advantages of wealth. The image of a free constitution was preserved with decent reverence. The Roman senate appeared to possess the sovereign authority, and devolved on the emperors all the executive powers of government.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Five Good Emperors' rule is considered the golden era of the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Commodus" title="Commodus">Commodus</a>, son of Marcus Aurelius, became emperor after his father's death. He is not counted as one of the Five Good Emperors. Firstly, this was due to his direct kinship with the latter emperor; in addition, he was militarily passive compared to his predecessors, who had frequently led their armies in person. Commodus usually participated in gladiatorial combats, which were frequently brutal and rough. He killed many citizens, and <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> identifies his reign as the beginning of Roman <a href="/wiki/Decadence" title="Decadence">decadence</a>: "(Rome has transformed) from a kingdom of gold to one of iron and rust."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Severan_dynasty">Severan dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Severan dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Commodus was killed by a conspiracy involving <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Aemilius_Laetus" title="Quintus Aemilius Laetus">Quintus Aemilius Laetus</a> and his wife Marcia in late 192 AD. The following year is known as the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_the_Five_Emperors" title="Year of the Five Emperors">Year of the Five Emperors</a>, during which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helvius_Pertinax" title="Helvius Pertinax">Helvius Pertinax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Didius_Julianus" title="Didius Julianus">Didius Julianus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">Pescennius Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clodius_Albinus" title="Clodius Albinus">Clodius Albinus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> held the imperial dignity. Pertinax, a member of the senate who had been one of Marcus Aurelius's right-hand men, was the choice of Laetus, and he ruled vigorously and judiciously. Laetus soon became jealous and instigated Pertinax's murder by the Praetorian Guard, who then auctioned the empire to the highest bidder, Didius Julianus, for 25,000 sesterces per man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[117]</a></sup> The people of Rome were appalled and appealed to the frontier legions to save them. The legions of three frontier provinces—<a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pannonia_Superior" title="Pannonia Superior">Pannonia Superior</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Syria" title="Roman Syria">Syria</a>—resented being excluded from the "<a href="/wiki/Donativum" title="Donativum">donative</a>" and replied by declaring their individual generals to be emperor. Lucius Septimius Severus Geta, the Pannonian commander, bribed the opposing forces, pardoned the Praetorian Guards and installed himself as emperor. He and his successors governed with the legions' support. The changes on <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a> and military expenditures were the root of the financial crisis that marked the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Septimius_Severus">Septimius Severus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Septimius Severus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Septimusseverustondo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="756" data-file-width="748" decoding="async" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Septimusseverustondo.jpg/200px-Septimusseverustondo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Septimusseverustondo.jpg/300px-Septimusseverustondo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Septimusseverustondo.jpg/400px-Septimusseverustondo.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Septimusseverustondo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Severan_Tondo" title="Severan Tondo">Severan Tondo</a>, c. 199, Severus, Julia Domna, Caracalla and Geta, whose face is erased</div></div></div>
<p>Severus was enthroned after invading Rome and having <a href="/wiki/Didius_Julianus" title="Didius Julianus">Didius Julianus</a> killed. His two other rivals, <a href="/wiki/Pescennius_Niger" title="Pescennius Niger">Pescennius Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clodius_Albinus" title="Clodius Albinus">Clodius Albinus</a>, were both hailed by other factions as <i>Imperator</i>. Severus quickly subdued Niger in <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and promised to Albinus the title of Caesar (which meant he would be a co-emperor).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[118]</a></sup> However, Severus betrayed Albinus by blaming him for a plot against his life. Severus marched to Gaul and defeated Albinus. For these acts, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Machiavelli" title="Machiavelli">Machiavelli</a> said that Severus was "a ferocious lion and a clever fox".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severus attempted to revive totalitarianism and, addressing the Roman people and Senate, praised the severity and cruelty of Marius and Sulla, which worried the senators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[120]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a> invaded Roman territory, Severus waged war against that country and seized the cities of <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">Nisibis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a>. Reaching <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a>, the Parthian capital, he ordered plundering and his army slew and captured many people. Notwithstanding this military success, Severus failed in invading <a href="/wiki/Hatra" title="Hatra">Hatra</a>, a rich Arabian city. Severus killed his legate, who was gaining respect from the legions; and his soldiers fell victim to famine. After this disastrous campaign, he withdrew.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup> Severus also intended to vanquish the whole of Britannia. To achieve this, he <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_invasion_of_Caledonia_208%E2%80%93210" title="Roman invasion of Caledonia 208–210">waged war</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Caledonians" title="Caledonians">Caledonians</a>. After many casualties in the army due to the terrain and the barbarians' ambushes, Severus himself went to the field. However, he became ill and died in 211 AD, at the age of 65.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="From_Caracalla_to_Alexander_Severus">From Caracalla to Alexander Severus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: From Caracalla to Alexander Severus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3675" data-file-width="2450" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg/170px-Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg/255px-Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg/340px-Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caracalla_Musei_Capitolini_MC2310.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Capitoline_Museums" title="Capitoline Museums">Capitoline Museums</a>, Rome</div></div></div>
<p>Upon the death of Severus, his sons <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Publius_Septimius_Geta" title="Publius Septimius Geta">Geta</a> were made emperors. During their youth, their squabbles had divided Rome. In that same year Caracalla had his brother, a youth, assassinated in his mother's arms, and may have murdered 20,000 of Geta's followers. Like his father, Caracalla was warlike. He continued Severus' policy and gained respect from the legions. A cruel man, Caracalla was pursued by the guilt of his brother's murder. He ordered the death of people of his own circle, like his tutor, Cilo, and a friend of his father, <a href="/wiki/Papinian" title="Papinian">Papinian</a>.
</p><p>Knowing that the citizens of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> disliked him and were denigrating his character, Caracalla served a banquet for its notable citizens, after which his soldiers killed all the guests. From the security of the temple of Sarapis, he then directed an indiscriminate slaughter of Alexandria's people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[122]</a></sup> In 212, he issued the <a href="/wiki/Constitutio_Antoniniana" title="Constitutio Antoniniana">Edict of Caracalla</a>, giving full Roman citizenship to all free men living in the Empire, with the exception of the <i>dediticii</i>, people who had become subject to Rome through surrender in war, and freed slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dediticii_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-dediticii-125">[123]</a></sup> and at the same time raised the inheritance tax, levied only on Roman citizens, to ten percent. A report that a soothsayer had predicted that the <a href="/wiki/Praetorian_prefect" title="Praetorian prefect">Praetorian prefect</a> <a href="/wiki/Macrinus" title="Macrinus">Macrinus</a> and his son were to rule over the empire was dutifully sent to Caracalla. But the report fell into the hands of Macrinus, who felt he must act or die. Macrinus conspired to have Caracalla assassinated by one of his soldiers during a pilgrimage to the Temple of the Moon in Carrhae, in 217 AD.
</p><p>The incompetent Macrinus assumed power, but soon removed himself from Rome to the east and Antioch. His brief reign ended in 218, when the youngster Bassianus, high priest of the temple of the Sun at Emesa, and supposedly illegitimate son of Caracalla, was declared Emperor by the disaffected soldiers of Macrinus. Bribes gained Bassianus support from the legionaries and they fought against Macrinus and his Praetorian guards. He adopted the name of Antoninus but history has named him after his Sun god <a href="/wiki/Elagabalus" title="Elagabalus">Elagabalus</a>, represented on Earth in the form of a large black stone. An incompetent and lascivious ruler,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haywood2_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-haywood2-47">[45]</a></sup> Elagabalus offended all but his favourites. <a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a></i> give many accounts of his notorious extravagance. Elagabalus adopted his cousin <a href="/wiki/Severus_Alexander" title="Severus Alexander">Severus Alexander</a>, as Caesar, but subsequently grew jealous and attempted to assassinate him. However, the Praetorian guard preferred Alexander, murdered Elagabalus, dragged his mutilated corpse through the streets of Rome, and threw it into the Tiber. Severus Alexander then succeeded him. Alexander waged war against many foes, including the revitalized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> and also the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, who invaded Gaul. His losses generated dissatisfaction among his soldiers, and some of them murdered him during his Germanic campaign in 235 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crisis_of_the_Third_Century">Crisis of the Third Century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Crisis of the Third Century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1213" data-file-width="2050" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg/350px-Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg/525px-Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg/700px-Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ancient_Rome_271_AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Roman Empire suffered internal schisms, forming the <a href="/wiki/Palmyrene_Empire" title="Palmyrene Empire">Palmyrene Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Empire" title="Gallic Empire">Gallic Empire</a></div></div></div>
<p>A disastrous scenario emerged after the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Severus" title="Alexander Severus">Alexander Severus</a>: the Roman state was plagued by civil wars, external <a href="/wiki/Invasion" title="Invasion">invasions</a>, political chaos, <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Cyprian" title="Plague of Cyprian">pandemics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_collapse" title="Economic collapse">economic depression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haywood2_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-haywood2-47">[45]</a></sup> The old Roman values had fallen, and <a href="/wiki/Mithraism" title="Mithraism">Mithraism</a> and Christianity had begun to spread through the populace. Emperors were no longer men linked with nobility; they usually were born in lower-classes of distant parts of the Empire. These men rose to prominence through military ranks, and became emperors through civil wars.
</p><p>There were 26 emperors in a 49-year period, a signal of political instability. <a href="/wiki/Maximinus_Thrax" title="Maximinus Thrax">Maximinus Thrax</a> was the first ruler of that time, governing for just three years. Others ruled just for a few months, like <a href="/wiki/Gordian_I" title="Gordian I">Gordian I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gordian_II" title="Gordian II">Gordian II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Balbinus" title="Balbinus">Balbinus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hostilian" title="Hostilian">Hostilian</a>. The population and the frontiers were abandoned, since the emperors were mostly concerned with defeating rivals and establishing their power. The economy also suffered during that epoch. The massive military expenditures from the <a href="/wiki/Severan_dynasty" title="Severan dynasty">Severi</a> caused a devaluation of Roman coins. <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">Hyperinflation</a> came at this time as well. The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Cyprian" title="Plague of Cyprian">Plague of Cyprian</a> broke out in 250 and killed a huge portion of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon10_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon10-128">[126]</a></sup> In 260 AD, the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Syria_Palaestina" title="Syria Palaestina">Syria Palaestina</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aegyptus_(Roman_province)" title="Aegyptus (Roman province)">Egypt</a> separated from the rest of the Roman state to form the <a href="/wiki/Palmyrene_Empire" title="Palmyrene Empire">Palmyrene Empire</a>, ruled by Queen <a href="/wiki/Zenobia" title="Zenobia">Zenobia</a> and centered on <a href="/wiki/Palmyra" title="Palmyra">Palmyra</a>. In that same year the <a href="/wiki/Gallic_Empire" title="Gallic Empire">Gallic Empire</a> was created by <a href="/wiki/Postumus" title="Postumus">Postumus</a>, retaining Britannia and Gaul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[127]</a></sup> These countries separated from Rome after the capture of emperor <a href="/wiki/Valerian_(emperor)" title="Valerian (emperor)">Valerian</a> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid" title="Sassanid">Sassanids</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, the first Roman ruler to be captured by his enemies; it was a humiliating fact for the Romans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon10_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon10-128">[126]</a></sup> The crisis began to recede during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Gothicus" title="Claudius Gothicus">Claudius Gothicus</a> (268–270), who <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Naissus" title="Battle of Naissus">defeated</a> the <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Gothic</a> invaders, and <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> (271–275), who reconquered both the Gallic and Palmyrene Empires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[128]</a></sup> The crisis was overcome during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a>.
</p>
<h2><span id="Empire_.E2.80.93_The_Tetrarchy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_–_The_Tetrarchy">Empire – The Tetrarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Empire – The Tetrarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diocletian">Diocletian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Diocletian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiocletianusFollis-transparent.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="263" data-file-width="253" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/DiocletianusFollis-transparent.png/220px-DiocletianusFollis-transparent.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/48/DiocletianusFollis-transparent.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiocletianusFollis-transparent.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Roman <a href="/wiki/Follis" title="Follis">follis</a> depicting the profile of <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 284 AD, Diocletian was hailed as Imperator by the eastern army. Diocletian healed the empire from the crisis, by political and economic shifts. A new form of government was established: the <a href="/wiki/Tetrarchy" title="Tetrarchy">Tetrarchy</a>. The Empire was divided among four emperors, two in the West and two in the East. The first tetrarchs were Diocletian (in the East), <a href="/wiki/Maximian" title="Maximian">Maximian</a> (in the West), and two junior emperors, <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a> (in the East) and <a href="/wiki/Constantius_Chlorus" title="Constantius Chlorus">Flavius Constantius</a> (in the West). To adjust the economy, Diocletian made several tax reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diocletian expelled the Persians who plundered Syria and conquered some barbarian tribes with Maximian. He adopted many behaviors of Eastern monarchs, like wearing pearls and golden sandals and robes. Anyone in the presence of the emperor had now to prostrate himself—a common act in the East, but never practiced in Rome before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[130]</a></sup> Diocletian did not use a disguised form of Republic, as the other emperors since <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> had done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[131]</a></sup> Between 290 and 330, half a dozen new capitals had been established by the members of the Tetrarchy, officially or not: Antioch, Nicomedia, Thessalonike, Sirmium, Milan, and Trier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[132]</a></sup> Diocletian was also responsible for a significant Christian persecution. In 303 he and <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a> started the persecution and ordered the destruction of all the Christian churches and scripts and forbade Christian worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[133]</a></sup> Diocletian abdicated in 305 AD together with Maximian, thus, he was the first Roman emperor to resign. His reign ended the traditional form of imperial rule, the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Principate</a> (from <a href="/wiki/Princeps" title="Princeps">princeps</a>) and started the Tetrarchy.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3824" data-file-width="5468" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg/220px-Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg/330px-Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg/440px-Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trier_Konstantinbasilika_BW_2017-06-16_14-07-56.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aula_Palatina" title="Aula Palatina">Aula Palatina</a> of <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, Germany (then part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gallia_Belgica" title="Gallia Belgica">Gallia Belgica</a>), a <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">Christian</a> <a href="/wiki/Basilica" title="Basilica">basilica</a> built during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> (r. 306–337 AD)</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Constantine_and_Christianity">Constantine and Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Constantine and Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine</a> assumed the empire as a tetrarch in 306. He conducted many wars against the other tetrarchs. Firstly he defeated <a href="/wiki/Maxentius" title="Maxentius">Maxentius</a> in 312. In 313, he issued the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a>, which granted liberty for Christians to profess their religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[134]</a></sup> Constantine was converted to Christianity, enforcing the Christian faith. He began the Christianization of the Empire and of Europe—a process concluded by the Catholic Church in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. He was defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alamanni" title="Alamanni">Alamanni</a> during 306–308. In 324 he defeated another tetrarch, <a href="/wiki/Licinius" title="Licinius">Licinius</a>, and controlled all the empire, as it was before <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a>. To celebrate his victories and Christianity's relevance, he rebuilt <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a> and renamed it Nova Roma ("New Rome"); but the city soon gained the informal name of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> ("City of Constantine").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>The reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_the_Apostate" title="Julian the Apostate">Julian</a>, who under the influence of his adviser <a href="/wiki/Mardonius_(philosopher)" title="Mardonius (philosopher)">Mardonius</a> attempted to restore <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Classical Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_religion" title="Hellenistic religion">Hellenistic religion</a>, only briefly interrupted the succession of Christian emperors. Constantinople served as a new capital for the Empire. In fact, Rome had lost its central importance since the Crisis of the Third Century—<a href="/wiki/Mediolanum" title="Mediolanum">Mediolanum</a> was the western capital from 286 to 330, until the reign of <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a>, when <a href="/wiki/Ravenna" title="Ravenna">Ravenna</a> was made capital, in the 5th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[136]</a></sup> Constantine's administrative and monetary reforms, that reunited the Empire under one emperor, and rebuilt the city of Byzantium changed the high period of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">ancient world</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Fall of the Western Roman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">Fall of the Western Roman Empire</a></div>
<p>In the late 4th and 5th centuries the Western Empire entered a critical stage which terminated with the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of the Western Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[137]</a></sup> Under the last emperors of the <a href="/wiki/Constantinian_dynasty" title="Constantinian dynasty">Constantinian dynasty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Valentinianic_dynasty" title="Valentinianic dynasty">Valentinianic dynasty</a>, Rome lost decisive battles against the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarians</a>: in 363, emperor <a href="/wiki/Julian_(emperor)" title="Julian (emperor)">Julian the Apostate</a> was killed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Samarra" title="Battle of Samarra">Battle of Samarra</a>, against the Persians and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adrianople" title="Battle of Adrianople">Battle of Adrianople</a> cost the life of emperor <a href="/wiki/Valens" title="Valens">Valens</a> (364–378); the victorious <a href="/wiki/Goths" title="Goths">Goths</a> were never expelled from the Empire nor assimilated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[138]</a></sup> The next emperor, <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a> (379–395), gave even more force to the Christian faith, and after his death, the Empire was divided into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>, ruled by <a href="/wiki/Arcadius" title="Arcadius">Arcadius</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, commanded by <a href="/wiki/Honorius_(emperor)" title="Honorius (emperor)">Honorius</a>, both of which were Theodosius' sons.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>

style="width:118px;max-width:118px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:158px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1171" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg/116px-Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg/174px-Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg/232px-Alaric_entering_Athens.jpg 2x" width="116"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:91px;max-width:91px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:158px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sack_of_Rome_by_the_Visigoths_on_24_August_410_by_JN_Sylvestre_1890.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1498" data-file-width="841" decoding="async" height="159"
