For example, what we call February 6, the Romans called <i>ante diem VIII idus Februarias</i> (inclusively the eighth day before the ides of February, which was February 13).
</p><p>The Roman year originally had ten months from Martius (March) to December, with the winter period not included in the calendar. The first four months were named after gods (Martius, Aprilis, Maius, Junius) and the others were numbered (Quintilis, Sextilis, September, October, November, and December). <a href="/wiki/Numa_Pompilius" title="Numa Pompilius">Numa Pompilius</a>, the second king of Rome (716–673 BC), is said to have introduced the months of January and February, both also named after gods, beginning the 12-month calendar still in use today. In 44 BC, the month Quintilis was renamed to Julius (July) after <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> and in 8 BC, Sextilis was renamed to Augustus (August) after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustus_Caesar" title="Augustus Caesar">Augustus Caesar</a>.
</p><p>The Romans had several ways of tracking years. One widespread way was the <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul#Consular_dating" title="Roman consul">consular dating</a>, which identified years by the two <a href="/wiki/Roman_consul" title="Roman consul">consuls</a> who ruled each year (their term was limited to one year); it was introduced in the Republic and continued to be used in the Empire, even though the consular post was reduced to a ceremonial one during that latter period. Another way, introduced in the late 3rd century AD, was counting years from the <a href="/wiki/Indiction" title="Indiction"><i>indictio</i></a>, a 15-year period based on the announcement of the delivery of food and other goods to the government. Another way, less popular but more similar to the way we currently count years, was <i><a href="/wiki/Ab_urbe_condita" title="Ab urbe condita">ab urbe condita</a></i>, which simply counted years from the mythical foundation of Rome in 753 BC. Thus, the year 653 BC would be 100 AUC, 1000 AD would be 1752 AUC (as there was no year 0 AD or BC) and so on.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_ancient_Rome" title="Culture of ancient Rome">Culture of ancient Rome</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="624" data-file-width="654" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg/250px-Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg/375px-Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg/500px-Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seven_Hills_of_Rome.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Seven_hills_of_Rome" title="Seven hills of Rome">seven hills of Rome</a></div></div></div>
<p>Life in ancient Rome revolved around the city of Rome, located on <a href="/wiki/Seven_hills_of_Rome" title="Seven hills of Rome">seven hills</a>. The city had a vast number of monumental structures like the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Trajan%27s_Forum" title="Trajan's Forum">Trajan's Forum</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a>. It had <a href="/wiki/Roman_theatre_(structure)" title="Roman theatre (structure)">theatres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(ancient_Greece)" title="Gymnasium (ancient Greece)">gymnasiums</a>, marketplaces, functional sewers, <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">bath complexes</a> complete with libraries and shops, and fountains with fresh drinking water supplied by hundreds of miles of <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">aqueducts</a>. Throughout the territory under the control of ancient Rome, residential architecture ranged from modest houses to <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">country villas</a>.
</p><p>In the capital city of Rome, there were <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial</a> <a href="/wiki/House" title="House">residences</a> on the elegant <a href="/wiki/Palatine_Hill" title="Palatine Hill">Palatine Hill</a>, from which the word <i>palace</i> derives. The low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebeian</a> and middle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equestrian_order" title="Equestrian order">equestrian</a> classes lived in the city center, packed into apartments, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insulae" title="Insulae">insulae</a>, which were almost like modern <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettos</a>. These areas, often built by upper class property owners to rent, were often centred upon <a href="/wiki/Collegium_(ancient_Rome)" title="Collegium (ancient Rome)">collegia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Taberna" title="Taberna">taberna</a>. These people, provided with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_supply_to_the_city_of_Rome" title="Grain supply to the city of Rome">free supply of grain</a>, and entertained by <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiatorial games</a>, were enrolled as <a href="/wiki/Patronage_in_ancient_Rome" title="Patronage in ancient Rome">clients of patrons</a> among the upper class <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patricians</a>, whose assistance they sought and whose interests they upheld.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a></div>
<p>The native <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> of the Romans was <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic language</a> the <a href="/wiki/Latin_grammar" title="Latin grammar">grammar of which</a> relies little on word order, conveying meaning through a system of <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixes</a> attached to <a href="/wiki/Word_stem" title="Word stem">word stems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[202]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">alphabet</a> was based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Italic_alphabet" title="Old Italic alphabet">Etruscan alphabet</a>, which was in turn based on the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[203]</a></sup> Although surviving <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a> consists almost entirely of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a>, an artificial and highly stylized and polished <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> from the 1st century BC, the <a href="/wiki/Spoken_language" title="Spoken language">spoken language</a> of the Roman Empire was <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a>, which significantly differed from Classical Latin in <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>, and eventually in pronunciation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[204]</a></sup> Speakers of Latin could understand both until the 7th century when spoken Latin began to diverge so much that 'Classical' or 'Good Latin' had to be learned as a second language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Latin remained the main written language of the Roman Empire, <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> came to be the language spoken by the well-educated elite, as most of the literature studied by Romans was written in Greek. In the eastern half of the Roman Empire, which later became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Roman_Empire" title="Eastern Roman Empire">Eastern Roman Empire</a>, Latin was never able to replace Greek, and after the death of Justinian, Greek became the official language of the Eastern government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998203_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998203-209">[206]</a></sup> The expansion of the Roman Empire spread Latin throughout Europe, and Vulgar Latin evolved into <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> in different locations, gradually shifting into many distinct <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Religion in ancient Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_temple" title="Roman temple">Roman temple</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great_and_Christianity" title="Constantine the Great and Christianity">Constantine the Great and Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">State church of the Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2680" data-file-width="2458" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg/220px-Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg/330px-Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg/440px-Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pompeii_-_Casa_dei_Vettii_-_Ixion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Punishment of <a href="/wiki/Ixion" title="Ixion">Ixion</a>: in the center is <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a> holding the <a href="/wiki/Caduceus" title="Caduceus">caduceus</a> and on the right <a href="/wiki/Juno_(mythology)" title="Juno (mythology)">Juno</a> sits on her throne. Behind her <a href="/wiki/Iris_(mythology)" title="Iris (mythology)">Iris</a> stands and gestures. On the left is <a href="/wiki/Vulcan_(mythology)" title="Vulcan (mythology)">Vulcan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a> figure) standing behind the wheel, manning it, with Ixion already tied to it. <a href="/wiki/Nephele" title="Nephele">Nephele</a> sits at Mercury's feet; a Roman fresco from the eastern wall of the <a href="/wiki/Triclinium" title="Triclinium">triclinium</a> in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_the_Vettii" title="House of the Vettii">House of the Vettii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pompeian_Styles" title="Pompeian Styles">Fourth Style</a> (60–79 AD).</div></div></div>
<p>Archaic <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman religion</a>, at least concerning the gods, was made up not of written <a href="/wiki/Narrative" title="Narrative">narratives</a>, but rather of complex interrelations between gods and humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[207]</a></sup> Unlike in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, the gods were not personified, but were vaguely defined sacred spirits called <i><a href="/wiki/Numen" title="Numen">numina</a></i>. Romans also believed that every person, place or thing had its own <i><a href="/wiki/Genius_(mythology)" title="Genius (mythology)">genius</a></i>, or divine soul. During the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman religion</a> was organized under a strict system of priestly offices, which were held by men of senatorial rank. The College of Pontifices was uppermost body in this hierarchy, and its chief priest, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" title="Pontifex Maximus">Pontifex Maximus</a></i>, was the head of the state religion. <a href="/wiki/Flamen" title="Flamen">Flamens</a> took care of the cults of various gods, while <a href="/wiki/Augur" title="Augur">augurs</a> were trusted with taking the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auspice" title="Auspice">auspices</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rex_Sacrorum" title="Rex Sacrorum">sacred king</a> took on the religious responsibilities of the deposed kings. In the Roman Empire, deceased emperors who had ruled well were deified by their successors and the Senate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[208]</a></sup> and the formalized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_cult_(ancient_Rome)" title="Imperial cult (ancient Rome)">imperial cult</a> became increasingly prominent.
</p><p>As contact with the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> increased, the old <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman gods</a> became increasingly associated with <a href="/wiki/List_of_Greek_mythological_figures" title="List of Greek mythological figures">Greek gods</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willis_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-willis-212">[209]</a></sup> Thus, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> was perceived to be the same deity as <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars_(mythology)" title="Mars (mythology)">Mars</a> became associated with <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a> with <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>. The Roman gods also assumed the attributes and mythologies of these Greek gods. Under the Empire, the Romans absorbed the mythologies of their conquered subjects, often leading to situations in which the temples and priests of traditional Italian deities existed side by side with those of foreign gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[210]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beginning with Emperor <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> in the 1st century AD, Roman official policy towards Christianity was negative, and at some points, simply being a Christian could be punishable by death. Under Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians" title="Persecution of Christians">persecution of Christians</a> reached its peak. However, it became an officially supported religion in the Roman state under Diocletian's successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a>, with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> in 313, and quickly became dominant. All religions except Christianity were prohibited in 391 AD by an edict of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics_and_morality">Ethics and morality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Ethics and morality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like many ancient cultures, concepts of ethics and morality, while sharing some commonalities with modern society, differed greatly in several important ways. Because ancient civilizations like Rome were under constant threat of attack from marauding tribes, their culture was necessarily militaristic with martial skills being a prized attribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuffPo:_Bread_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-HuffPo:_Bread-215">[212]</a></sup> Whereas modern societies consider <a href="/wiki/Compassion" title="Compassion">compassion</a> a virtue, Roman society considered compassion a vice, a moral defect. Indeed, one of the primary purposes of the gladiatorial games was to inoculate Roman citizens from this weakness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HuffPo:_Bread_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-HuffPo:_Bread-215">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[214]</a></sup> Romans instead prized virtues such as courage and conviction (<i>virtus</i>), a sense of duty to one's people, moderation and avoiding excess (<i>moderatio</i>), forgiveness and understanding (<i>clementia</i>), fairness (<i>severitas</i>), and loyalty (<i><a href="/wiki/Pietas" title="Pietas">pietas</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contrary to popular descriptions, Roman society had well-established and restrictive norms related to sexuality, though as with many societies, the lion's share of the responsibilities fell on women. Women were generally expected to be monogamous having only a single husband during their life (<i>univira</i>), though this was much less regarded by the elite, especially under the empire. Women were expected to be modest in public avoiding any provocative appearance and to demonstrate absolute fidelity to their husbands (<i>pudicitia</i>). Indeed, wearing a veil was a common expectation to preserve modesty. Sex outside of marriage was generally frowned upon for men and women and indeed was made illegal during the imperial period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup> Nevertheless, prostitution was seen entirely differently and indeed was an accepted and regulated practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dillon_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dillon-220">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Art.2C_music_and_literature"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Art,_music_and_literature">Art, music and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Art, music and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Rome" title="Music of ancient Rome">Music of ancient Rome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_sculpture" title="Roman sculpture">Roman sculpture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_ancient_Rome" title="Theatre of ancient Rome">Theatre of ancient Rome</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Art_collection_in_ancient_Rome" title="Art collection in ancient Rome">Art collection in ancient Rome</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome_(painting)" title="Ancient Rome (painting)">Ancient Rome (painting)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Villa_of_the_Mysteries_(Pompeii)_-_frescos_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3004" data-file-width="2922" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg/220px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg/330px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg/440px-Villa_of_the_Mysteries_%28Pompeii%29_-_frescos_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Villa_of_the_Mysteries_(Pompeii)_-_frescos_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frescoes from the <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Mysteries" title="Villa of the Mysteries">Villa of the Mysteries</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, Italy, <a href="/wiki/Roman_art" title="Roman art">Roman artwork</a> dated to the mid-1st century BC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2670" data-file-width="2712" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG/220px-P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG/330px-P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG/440px-P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P._Fannius_Synistor_anagoria_links.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woman playing a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kithara" title="Kithara">kithara</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Villa_Boscoreale" title="Villa Boscoreale">Villa Boscoreale</a>, 40–30 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Roman painting styles show <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> influences, and surviving examples are primarily <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">frescoes</a> used to adorn the walls and ceilings of <a href="/wiki/Villa_rustica" title="Villa rustica">country villas</a>, though Roman literature includes mentions of paintings on wood, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>, and other materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352-221">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MetstuffonRpaint_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-MetstuffonRpaint-222">[219]</a></sup> Several examples of Roman painting have been found at <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, and from these art historians divide the history of Roman painting into <a href="/wiki/Pompeian_Styles" title="Pompeian Styles">four periods</a>.
</p><p>The first style of Roman painting was practiced from the early 2nd century BC to the early- or mid-1st century BC. It was mainly composed of imitations of <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> and <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">masonry</a>, though sometimes including depictions of mythological characters.
The second style began during the early 1st century BC and attempted to depict realistically three-dimensional architectural features and landscapes. The third style occurred during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> (27 BC – 14 AD), and rejected the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Realism_(visual_arts)" title="Realism (visual arts)">realism</a> of the second style in favor of simple ornamentation. A small architectural scene, landscape, or abstract design was placed in the center with a <a href="/wiki/Monochrome" title="Monochrome">monochrome</a> background. The fourth style, which began in the 1st century AD, depicted scenes from mythology, while retaining architectural details and abstract patterns.
</p><p>Portrait sculpture during the period<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2014)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> utilized youthful and classical proportions, evolving later into a mixture of realism and <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>. During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonines" title="Antonines">Antonine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Severan_dynasty" title="Severan dynasty">Severan</a> periods, ornate hair and bearding, with deep cutting and drilling, became popular. Advancements were also made in <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief sculptures</a>, usually depicting Roman victories.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">Latin literature</a> was, from its start, influenced heavily by Greek authors. Some of the earliest extant works are of historical <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epics</a> telling the early military history of Rome. As the Republic expanded, authors began to produce poetry, comedy, history, and <a href="/wiki/Tragedy" title="Tragedy">tragedy</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Rome" title="Music of ancient Rome">Roman music</a> was largely based on <a href="/wiki/Music_of_ancient_Greece" title="Music of ancient Greece">Greek music</a>, and played an important part in many aspects of Roman life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iClassics_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-iClassics-223">[220]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Military_of_ancient_Rome" title="Military of ancient Rome">Roman military</a>, musical instruments such as the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tuba#Latin" title="wikt:tuba">tuba</a></i> (a long trumpet) or the <i><a href="/wiki/Cornu_(horn)" title="Cornu (horn)">cornu</a></i> were used to give various commands, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Buccina" title="Buccina">buccina</a></i> (possibly a trumpet or horn) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Lituus" title="Lituus">lituus</a></i> (probably an elongated J-shaped instrument), were used in ceremonial capacities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins199889_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins199889-224">[221]</a></sup> Music was used in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_amphitheatre" title="Roman amphitheatre">Roman amphitheatres</a> between fights and in the <i><a href="/wiki/Odeon_(building)" title="Odeon (building)">odea</a></i>, and in these settings is known to have featured the <i>cornu</i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulis" title="Hydraulis">hydraulis</a></i> (a type of water organ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998349–350_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998349–350-225">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most religious rituals featured musical performances, with <i>tibiae</i> (double pipes) at sacrifices, <a href="/wiki/Cymbal" title="Cymbal">cymbals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tambourine" title="Tambourine">tambourines</a> at <a href="/wiki/Orgy" title="Orgy">orgiastic</a> <a href="/wiki/Cult_(religious_practice)" title="Cult (religious practice)">cults</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rattle_(percussion_instrument)" title="Rattle (percussion instrument)">rattles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hymns" title="Hymns">hymns</a> across the spectrum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998300_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998300-226">[223]</a></sup> Some music historians believe that music was used at almost all public ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iClassics_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-iClassics-223">[220]</a></sup> Music historians are not certain if Roman musicians made a significant contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">theory</a> or practice of music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iClassics_223-2"><a href="#cite_note-iClassics-223">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Graffiti" title="Graffiti">graffiti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brothel" title="Brothel">brothels</a>, paintings, and sculptures found in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herculaneum" title="Herculaneum">Herculaneum</a> suggest that the Romans had a sex-saturated culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Cuisine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_cuisine" title="Ancient Roman cuisine">Ancient Roman cuisine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome_and_wine" title="Ancient Rome and wine">Ancient Rome and wine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food_and_dining_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="Food and dining in the Roman Empire">Food and dining in the Roman Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno,_III_secolo_dc..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1647" data-file-width="1678" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG/220px-Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG/330px-Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG/440px-Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno%2C_III_secolo_dc..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosaico_di_un_giovane_come_mese_di_giugno,_III_secolo_dc..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A boy holding a platter of fruits and what may be a bucket of crabs, in a kitchen with fish and <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, on the June panel from a mosaic depicting the months (3rd century)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[225]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Roman cuisine changed over the long duration of this ancient civilization. Dietary habits were affected by the influence of Greek culture, the political changes from <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Kingdom</a> to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Republic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Empire</a>, and the Empire's enormous expansion, which exposed Romans to many new, provincial culinary habits and cooking techniques. In the beginning the differences between social classes were relatively small, but disparities evolved with the Empire's growth. Men and women drank wine with their meals, a tradition that has been carried through to the present day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[226]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingredients">Ingredients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Ingredients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The ancient Roman diet included many items that are staples of <a href="/wiki/Italian_cuisine" title="Italian cuisine">modern Italian cooking</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> discussed more than 30 varieties of <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olive</a>, 40 kinds of <a href="/wiki/Pear" title="Pear">pear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ficus" title="Ficus">figs</a> (native and imported from Africa and the eastern provinces), and a wide variety of <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Carrot" title="Carrot">carrots</a> (of different colors, but not orange<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[227]</a></sup>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Celery" title="Celery">celery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Garlic" title="Garlic">garlic</a>, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flower_bulb" title="Flower bulb">flower bulbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cabbage" title="Cabbage">cabbage</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Brassica" title="Brassica">brassicas</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Kale" title="Kale">kale</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broccoli" title="Broccoli">broccoli</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lettuce" title="Lettuce">lettuce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Endive" title="Endive">endive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radish" title="Radish">radishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turnip" title="Turnip">turnips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parsnip" title="Parsnip">parsnips</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beet" title="Beet">beets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_peas" title="Green peas">green peas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chard" title="Chard">chard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardoon" title="Cardoon">cardoons</a>, olives, and <a href="/wiki/Cucumber" title="Cucumber">cucumber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatrickFaas_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-PatrickFaas-231">[228]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, some foods now considered characteristic of modern Italian cuisine were not used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhyllisPray_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-PhyllisPray-232">[229]</a></sup> In particular, <a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eggplant" title="Eggplant">eggplant</a> (aubergine) were introduced later from the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">tomatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capsicum" title="Capsicum">capsicum</a> peppers, and <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (the modern source of <a href="/wiki/Polenta" title="Polenta">polenta</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PatrickFaas_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-PatrickFaas-231">[228]</a></sup> only appeared in Europe following the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">Columbian Exchange</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhyllisPray_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-PhyllisPray-232">[229]</a></sup> The Romans knew of <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, but it was very rarely available to them. There were also few <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> fruits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PhyllisPray_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-PhyllisPray-232">[229]</a></sup> Lemons were known in Italy from the second century AD but were not widely cultivated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>Butcher's meat such as <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> was an uncommon luxury. The most popular meat was <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaguelonneToussaintSamat_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaguelonneToussaintSamat-234">[231]</a></sup> Fish was more common than meat, with a sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> and large-scale industries devoted to <a href="/wiki/Oyster_farming" title="Oyster farming">oyster farming</a>. The Romans also engaged in <a href="/wiki/Heliciculture" title="Heliciculture">snail farming</a> and oak grub farming. Some fish were greatly esteemed and fetched high prices, such as <a href="/wiki/Mullet_(fish)" title="Mullet (fish)">mullet</a> raised in the fishery at <a href="/wiki/Cosa" title="Cosa">Cosa</a>, and "elaborate means were invented to assure its freshness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-John-235">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Meals">Meals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Meals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Traditionally, a <a href="/wiki/Breakfast" title="Breakfast">breakfast</a> called <i>ientaculum</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtmanJohnAncient_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArtmanJohnAncient-236">[233]</a></sup> was served at dawn. At mid-day to early afternoon, Romans ate <i><a href="/wiki/Cena" title="Cena">cena</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtmanJohnAncient_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-ArtmanJohnAncient-236">[233]</a></sup> the main meal of the day, and at nightfall a light supper called <i>vesperna</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artman,_John_page_26_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Artman,_John_page_26-237">[234]</a></sup> With the increased importation of foreign foods, the <i>cena</i> grew larger in size and included a wider range of foods. Thus, it gradually shifted to the evening, while the <i>vesperna</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artman,_John_page_26_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-Artman,_John_page_26-237">[234]</a></sup> was abandoned completely over the course of the years. The mid-day meal <i>prandium</i> became a light meal to hold one over until <i>cena</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArtmanJohnAncient_236-2"><a href="#cite_note-ArtmanJohnAncient-236">[233]</a></sup> Among the lower classes of the Roman society, these changes were less pronounced as the traditional routines corresponded closely to the daily rhythms of manual labour.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Games_and_recreation">Games and recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Games and recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_physical_training_and_fitness" title="History of physical training and fitness">History of physical training and fitness</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG/220px-Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG/330px-Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG/440px-Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Villa_romana_di_Piazza_Armerina_-_Sicilia.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mosaic of "Big Game" hunters, Sicily, 4th century AD</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Retiarius_stabs_secutor_(color).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="260" data-file-width="273" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Retiarius_stabs_secutor_%28color%29.jpg/220px-Retiarius_stabs_secutor_%28color%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/01/Retiarius_stabs_secutor_%28color%29.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Retiarius_stabs_secutor_(color).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gladiator combat was strictly a spectator sport. This mosaic shows combatants and referee, from the villa at <a href="/wiki/Nennig" title="Nennig">Nennig</a>, Germany, c. 2nd–3rd century AD.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2747" data-file-width="4227" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG/220px-Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG/330px-Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG/440px-Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Villa_romana_bikini_girls.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "<a href="/wiki/Bikini" title="Bikini">bikini</a> girls" <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>, showing women playing sports, from the <a href="/wiki/Villa_Romana_del_Casale" title="Villa Romana del Casale">Villa Romana del Casale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">Roman province</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sicilia_(Roman_province)" title="Sicilia (Roman province)">Sicilia</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>), 4th century AD</div></div></div>
<p>The youth of Rome had several forms of athletic play and exercise. Play for boys was supposed to prepare them for active military service, such as <a href="/wiki/Jumping" title="Jumping">jumping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wrestling" title="Wrestling">wrestling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boxing" title="Boxing">boxing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Racing" title="Racing">racing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassononsports_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cassononsports-238">[235]</a></sup> In the countryside, pastimes for the wealthy also included fishing and hunting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPQRonline_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-SPQRonline-239">[236]</a></sup> The Romans also had several forms of ball playing, including one resembling <a href="/wiki/American_handball" title="American handball">handball</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassononsports_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cassononsports-238">[235]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dice_game" title="Dice game">Dice games</a>, <a href="/wiki/Board_game" title="Board game">board games</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gambling" title="Gambling">gamble games</a> were popular pastimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassononsports_238-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cassononsports-238">[235]</a></sup> For the wealthy, dinner parties presented an opportunity for entertainment, sometimes featuring music, dancing, and poetry readings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352-221">[218]</a></sup> The majority, less well-off, sometimes enjoyed similar parties through clubs or associations, but for most Romans, recreational dining usually meant patronizing <a href="/wiki/Tavern" title="Tavern">taverns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352_221-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352-221">[218]</a></sup>Children entertained themselves with toys and such games as <a href="/wiki/Leapfrog" title="Leapfrog">leapfrog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPQRonline_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-SPQRonline-239">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352_221-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAdkinsAdkins1998350–352-221">[218]</a></sup>
</p><p>Public games and spectacles were sponsored by leading Romans who wished to advertise their generosity and court popular approval; in Rome or its provinces, this usually meant the emperor or his governors. Venues in Rome and the provinces were developed specifically for public games. Rome's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colisseum" title="Colisseum">Colisseum</a> was built in the Imperial era to host, among other events, <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiatorial</a> combats. These combats had begun as funeral games around the 4th century BC, and became popular spectator events in the late Republic and Empire. Gladiators had an exotic and inventive variety of arms and armour. They sometimes fought to the death, but more often to an adjudicated victory, dependent on a referee's decision. The outcome was usually in keeping with the mood of the watching crowd. Shows of exotic animals were popular in their own right; but sometimes animals were pitted against human beings, either armed professionals or unarmed criminals who had been condemned to a spectacular and theatrical public death in the arena. Some of these encounters were based on episodes from Roman or Greek mythology.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chariot_racing" title="Chariot racing">Chariot racing</a> was extremely popular among all classes. In Rome, these races were usually held at the <a href="/wiki/Circus_Maximus" title="Circus Maximus">Circus Maximus</a>, which had been purpose-built for chariot and horse-racing and, as Rome's largest public place, was also used for festivals and animal shows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[237]</a></sup> It could seat around 150,000 people;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[238]</a></sup> The charioteers raced in teams, identified by their colours. The track was divided lengthwise by a barrier that contained obelisks, temples, statues and lap-counters. The best seats were at the track-side, close to the action; they were reserved for Senators. Behind them sat the <a href="/wiki/Equites" title="Equites">equites</a> (knights), and behind the knights were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plebs" title="Plebs">plebs</a> (commoners) and non-citizens. The donor of the games sat on a high platform in the stands alongside images of the gods, visible to all. Large sums were bet on the outcomes of races. Some Romans offered prayers and sacrifices on behalf of their favourites, or illegally laid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curse_tablets" title="Curse tablets">curses</a> on the opposing teams, and some aficionados were members of extremely, even violently partisan circus factions.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_technology" title="Ancient Roman technology">Ancient Roman technology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg/220px-Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg/330px-Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg/440px-Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pont_du_Gard_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pont_du_Gard" title="Pont du Gard">Pont du Gard</a> in France is a <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">Roman aqueduct</a> built in c. 19 BC. It is a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient Rome boasted impressive technological feats, using many advancements that were lost in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> and not rivaled again until the 19th and 20th centuries. An example of this is <a href="/wiki/Insulated_glazing" title="Insulated glazing">insulated glazing</a>, which was not invented again until the 1930s. Many practical Roman innovations were adopted from earlier Greek designs. Advancements were often divided and based on craft. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_(profession)" title="Trade (profession)">Artisans</a> guarded technologies as <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secrets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[239]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_engineering" title="Ancient Roman engineering">Roman civil engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_military_engineering" title="Roman military engineering">military engineering</a> constituted a large part of Rome's technological superiority and legacy, and contributed to the construction of hundreds of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_road" title="Roman road">roads</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_bridge" title="Roman bridge">bridges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_aqueduct" title="Roman aqueduct">aqueducts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">public baths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_theatre_(structure)" title="Roman theatre (structure)">theatres</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_amphitheatre" title="Roman amphitheatre">arenas</a>. Many monuments, such as the <a href="/wiki/Colosseum" title="Colosseum">Colosseum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pont_du_Gard" title="Pont du Gard">Pont du Gard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Pantheon</a>, remain as testaments to Roman engineering and culture.
</p><p>The Romans were renowned for their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" title="Roman architecture">architecture</a>, which is grouped with Greek traditions into "<a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">Classical architecture</a>". Although there were many differences from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architecture_of_ancient_Greece" title="Architecture of ancient Greece">Greek architecture</a>, Rome borrowed heavily from Greece in adhering to strict, formulaic building designs and proportions. Aside from two new <a href="/wiki/Classical_order" title="Classical order">orders</a> of columns, <a href="/wiki/Composite_order" title="Composite order">composite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuscan_order" title="Tuscan order">Tuscan</a>, and from the <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a>, which was derived from the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arch</a>, Rome had relatively few architectural innovations until the end of the Republic.
</p><p>In the 1st century BC, Romans started to use <a href="/wiki/Roman_concrete" title="Roman concrete">Roman concrete</a> widely. Concrete was <a href="/wiki/Roman_architectural_revolution" title="Roman architectural revolution">invented</a> in the late 3rd century BC. It was a powerful cement derived from <a href="/wiki/Pozzolana" title="Pozzolana">pozzolana</a>, and soon supplanted <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> as the chief Roman building material and allowed many daring architectural forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[240]</a></sup> Also in the 1st century BC, <a href="/wiki/Vitruvius" title="Vitruvius">Vitruvius</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/De_architectura" title="De architectura">De architectura</a></i>, possibly the first complete treatise on architecture in history. In the late 1st century BC, Rome also began to use <a href="/wiki/Glassblowing" title="Glassblowing">glassblowing</a> soon after its invention in Syria about 50 BC. <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">Mosaics</a> took the Empire by storm after samples were retrieved during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a>'s campaigns in Greece.
</p><p>The Romans also largely built using timber, causing a rapid decline of the woodlands surrounding Rome and in much of the Apennine Mountains due to the demand for wood for construction, shipbuilding and fire. The first evidence of long-distance wood trading come from the discovery of wood planks, felled between A.D. 40 and 60, coming from the Jura mountains in northeastern France and ending up more than 1,055 miles away, in the foundations of a lavish portico that was part of a vast wealthy patrician villa, in Central Rome. It is suggested that timber, around 4 meters long, came up to Rome via the Tiber River via ships traveling across the Mediterranean Sea from the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Sa%C3%B4ne" title="Saône">Saône</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rh%C3%B4ne" title="Rhône">Rhône</a> rivers in what is now the city of <a href="/wiki/Lyon" title="Lyon">Lyon</a> in present-day France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[241]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg/220px-Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg/330px-Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg/440px-Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Appia_antica_2-7-05_048.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Appian_Way" title="Appian Way">Appian Way</a> (<i>Via Appia</i>), a road connecting the city of Rome to the southern parts of <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, remains usable even today</div></div></div>
<p>With solid foundations and good drainage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[242]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Roman_roads" title="Roman roads">Roman roads</a> were known for their durability and many segments of the Roman road system were still in use a thousand years after the fall of Rome. The construction of a vast and efficient travel network throughout the Empire dramatically increased Rome's power and influence. They allowed <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">Roman legions</a> to be deployed rapidly, with predictable marching times between key points of the empire, no matter the season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[243]</a></sup> These highways also had enormous economic significance, solidifying Rome's role as a trading crossroads—the origin of the saying "all roads lead to Rome". The Roman government maintained a system of way stations, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Cursus_publicus" title="Cursus publicus">cursus publicus</a></i>, that provided refreshments to couriers at regular intervals along the roads and established a system of horse relays allowing a dispatch to travel up to 80 km (50 mi) a day.
</p><p>The Romans constructed numerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(watercourse)" title="Aqueduct (watercourse)">aqueducts</a> to supply water to cities and industrial sites and to aid in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_agriculture" title="Roman agriculture">their agriculture</a>. By the third century, the city of Rome was supplied by <a href="/wiki/List_of_aqueducts_in_the_city_of_Rome" title="List of aqueducts in the city of Rome">11 aqueducts</a> with a combined length of 450 km (280 mi). Most aqueducts were constructed below the surface, with only small portions above ground supported by arches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HDCLA_Aqueduct_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-HDCLA_Aqueduct-247">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB11Aqueduct_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB11Aqueduct-248">[245]</a></sup> Sometimes, where valleys deeper than 500 m (1,640 ft) had to be crossed, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inverted_siphon" title="Inverted siphon">inverted siphons</a> were used to convey water across a valley.<sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_waterhistory"><a class="external autonumber" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Rome#endnote_waterhistory">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Romans also made major advancements in <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>. Romans were particularly famous for their public <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">baths</a>, called <i><a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">thermae</a></i>, which were used for both hygienic and social purposes. Many Roman houses came to have <a href="/wiki/Flush_toilet" title="Flush toilet">flush toilets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tap_water" title="Tap water">indoor plumbing</a>, and a complex <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_sewer" title="Sanitary sewer">sewer</a> system, the <i><a href="/wiki/Cloaca_Maxima" title="Cloaca Maxima">Cloaca Maxima</a></i>, was used to drain the local <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a> and carry waste into the Tiber river.
</p><p>Some historians have speculated that lead pipes in the sewer and plumbing systems led to widespread <a href="/wiki/Lead_poisoning" title="Lead poisoning">lead poisoning</a>, which contributed to the decline in <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a> and general decay of Roman society leading up to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">fall of Rome</a>. However, lead content would have been minimized because the flow of water from aqueducts could not be shut off; it ran continuously through public and private outlets into the drains, and only a few taps were in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[246]</a></sup> Other authors have raised similar objections to this theory, also pointing out that Roman water pipes were thickly coated with deposits that would have prevented lead from leaching into the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grout2011_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grout2011-250">[247]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Legacy_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Legacy of the Roman Empire">Legacy of the Roman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Classics</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External video</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image" colspan="2"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1533" data-file-width="1218" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg/220px-ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg/330px-ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg/440px-ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ImageRomeArchofTitus02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arch of Titus</div></div></div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="video icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://smarthistory.khanacademy.org/ancient-rome-an-introduction.html" rel="nofollow">Ancient Rome</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smarth_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-smarth-251">[248]</a></sup> (13:47), <a href="/wiki/Smarthistory" title="Smarthistory">Smarthistory</a> at <a href="/wiki/Khan_Academy" title="Khan Academy">Khan Academy</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Ancient Rome is the progenitor of <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Western_civilization" title="Western civilization">Western civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[249]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(sociology)" title="Norm (sociology)">customs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">law</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_technology" title="Roman technology">technology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_architecture" title="Roman architecture">architecture</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Politics_of_Ancient_Rome" title="Politics of Ancient Rome">political system</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_military" title="Roman military">military</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_literature" title="Latin literature">literature</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">languages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">alphabet</a>, government and many factors and aspects of western civilization are all inherited from Roman advancements. The rediscovery of Roman culture revitalized Western civilization, playing a role in the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Age of Enlightenment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[250]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)#Genetic_studies" title="Latins (Italic tribe)">Latins (Italic tribe) § Genetic studies</a></div>
<p>A genetic study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> in November 2019 examined the genetic history of Rome from the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> up to modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191-254">[251]</a></sup> The Mesolithic inhabitants of Rome were determined to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Hunter_Gatherer" title="Western Hunter Gatherer">Western Hunter Gatherers</a> (WHGs), who were almost entirely replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_European_Farmer" title="Early European Farmer">Early European Farmers</a> (EEFs) around 6,000 BC coming from <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191–2_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191–2-255">[252]</a></sup> However, the authors observe that the EEFs studied carry a small amount of another component that is found at high levels in <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Iran#Neolithic_to_Chalcolithic" title="Prehistory of Iran">Neolithic Iranian</a> farmers and Caucasus hunter-gatherers (CHG),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBIAntonio2019_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCBIAntonio2019-256">[253]</a></sup> suggesting different or additional population contributions from <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near Eastern</a> farmers during the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic transition</a>, according to the authors.
</p><p>Between 2,900 BC and 900 BC, the EEF/WHG descended population of Rome was overwhelmed by peoples with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steppe_ancestry" title="Steppe ancestry">steppe ancestry</a> largely tracing their origin to the <a href="/wiki/Pontic%E2%80%93Caspian_steppe" title="Pontic–Caspian steppe">Pontic–Caspian steppe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191–2_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191–2-255">[252]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)" title="Latins (Italic tribe)">Latin</a> founding population of Rome which subsequently emerged carried the paternal <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R-M269" title="Haplogroup R-M269">haplogroup R-M269</a> at a minor but significant rate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.2019Table_S2_Sample_Information_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.2019Table_S2_Sample_Information-257">[254]</a></sup> and were of about 15-20% steppe ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191-2_Fig._2_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191-2_Fig._2-258">[255]</a></sup> However, two out of six individuals from Latin burials from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latium_vetus" title="Latium vetus">Latium vetus</a> were found to be a mixture of local Iron Age ancestry and an <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">ancient Near Eastern population</a> (best approximated by Bronze Age <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> or Iron Age <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> population). In addition, one out of four individuals from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscan</a> burials from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veio" title="Veio">Veio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Civitavecchia" title="Civitavecchia">Civitavecchia</a>, a female, was found to be a mixture of local Iron Age ancestry and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African" title="North African">North African</a> population (best approximated by <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Late Neolithic Moroccan</a>). Overall, the genetic differentiation between the <a href="/wiki/Latins_(Italic_tribe)" title="Latins (Italic tribe)">Latins</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscans" title="Etruscans">Etruscans</a> and the preceding proto-Villanovan population of Italy was found to be insignificant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCBIAntonio2019_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCBIAntonio2019-256">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>Examined individuals from Rome during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> (27 BCE – 300 CE) bore almost no genetic resemblance to Rome's founding populations, and were instead shifted towards the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a> and <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, largely overlapping with modern such as <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maltese_people" title="Maltese people">Maltese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greek_Cypriots" title="Greek Cypriots">Cypriot</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syrian" title="Syrian">Syrian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[256]</a></sup> The Imperial population of Rome was found to have been extremely diverse, with barely any of the examined individuals being of primarily western European ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wade_673_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wade_673-260">[257]</a></sup> It was suggested that the large population size and the presence of megacities in the east, such as <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, may have driven a net flow of people from east to west during antiquity; in addition, eastern ancestry could have reached Rome also through <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punics" title="Punics">Punic</a> diasporas that were established through colonies across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> prior to Roman Imperial expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20193–6_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20193–6-261">[258]</a></sup> During <a href="/wiki/Late_antiquity" title="Late antiquity">late antiquity</a>, Rome's population was drastically reduced as a result of political instability, epidemics and economic changes. Repeated invasions of <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbarians</a> brought European ancestry back into Rome, resulting in the loss of genetic link to the Eastern Mediterranean and Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wade_673_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wade_673-260">[257]</a></sup> By the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the people of Rome again genetically resembled European populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wade_673_260-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wade_673-260">[257]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Physical appearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As regards to the data on the <a href="/wiki/Biological_pigment" title="Biological pigment">pigmentation</a> of eyes, hair, and skin, the following results were obtained from the study on <a href="/wiki/Ancient_DNA" title="Ancient DNA">ancient DNA</a> of 11 individuals of the Iron Age/Republican period, coming from Latium and Abruzzo, and 27 individuals of Medieval/Early Modern period, coming from Latium.
</p><p>In the Iron Age/Republic period, the <a href="/wiki/Eye_color" title="Eye color">eye color</a> is blue in 27% of those examined and dark in the remaining 73%. Hair color is 9% <a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a>, or dark blond, and 91% dark brown or black. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_color" title="Skin color">skin color</a> is intermediate for 82%, intermediate or dark for 9%, and dark or very dark for the remaining 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tina_Saupe_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tina_Saupe-262">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, the following results were obtained for the Medieval/Early Modern period: the eye color is blue in 26% of those examined and dark in the remaining 74%. Hair color is 22% blond or dark blond, 11% <a href="/wiki/Red_hair" title="Red hair">red</a>, and 67% dark brown or black. The skin color is pale for 15%, intermediate for 68%, intermediate or dark for 10%, and dark or very dark for the remaining 7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tina_Saupe_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tina_Saupe-262">[259]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Misconceptions">Misconceptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Misconceptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are a number of individual misconceptions about the Roman period.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4320" data-file-width="3240" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg/170px-VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg/255px-VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg/340px-VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VomitoriumAtTriersRomanAmphitheatre2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i><a href="/wiki/Vomitorium" title="Vomitorium">vomitorium</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Trier_Amphitheater" title="Trier Amphitheater">Roman amphitheatre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_sculpture" title="Ancient Greek sculpture">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_sculpture" title="Roman sculpture">Roman sculptures</a> were <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_sculpture#Painting_of_sculpture" title="Ancient Greek sculpture">originally painted with bright colors</a>; they only appear white or grey today because the original pigments have deteriorated. Some well-preserved statues still bear traces of their original coloration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[260]</a></sup></li>
<li>There is no evidence that the <a href="/wiki/Roman_salute" title="Roman salute">Roman salute</a>, in which the arm is fully extended forwards or diagonally with fingers touching, was actually used in ancient Rome for greeting or any other purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[261]</a></sup> The idea that the salute was popular in ancient times originated in the 1784 painting <i><a href="/wiki/Oath_of_the_Horatii" title="Oath of the Horatii">Oath of the Horatii</a></i> by the French artist <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a>, which inspired later salutes, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_salute" title="Nazi salute">Nazi salute</a>.</li>
<li>Vomiting was not a regular part of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_cuisine#Table_culture" title="Ancient Roman cuisine">Roman dining customs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[262]</a></sup> In ancient Rome, the architectural feature called a <i><a href="/wiki/Vomitorium" title="Vomitorium">vomitorium</a></i> was the entranceway through which crowds entered and exited a stadium, not a special room used for purging food during meals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[263]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scipio_Aemilianus" title="Scipio Aemilianus">Scipio Aemilianus</a> did not plow over the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salting_the_earth" title="Salting the earth">sow it with salt</a> after defeating it in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[264]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> was not born via <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean section</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[265]</a></sup> Such a procedure would have been fatal to the mother at the time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[266]</a></sup> and historical evidence indicates Caesar's mother being alive during his own lifetime. Although the names are similar, the caesarean section was not named after Caesar, as is commonly believed; it is more likely related to "cease" and derived from the Latin verb <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/caedere" title="wikt:caedere">caedere</a></i>, meaning "to cut."</li>
<li>The death of the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Hypatia" title="Hypatia">Hypatia of Alexandria</a> at the hands of a mob of Christian monks in 415 was mainly a result of her involvement in a bitter political feud between her close friend and student <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orestes_(prefect)" title="Orestes (prefect)">Orestes</a>, the Roman prefect of Alexandria, and the bishop <a href="/wiki/Cyril_of_Alexandria" title="Cyril of Alexandria">Cyril</a>, not her religious views.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[267]</a></sup> Her death also had nothing to do with the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Alexandria" title="Library of Alexandria">Library of Alexandria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore2016_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore2016-271">[268]</a></sup> which had likely already ceased to exist centuries before Hypatia was born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theodore2016_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-Theodore2016-271">[268]</a></sup></li></ul>
