<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Roman_historiography" title="Roman historiography">Roman historiography</a></div>
<p>Although there has been a diversity of works on ancient Roman history, many of them are lost. As a result of this loss, there are gaps in Roman history, which are filled by unreliable works, such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Augusta" title="Historia Augusta">Historia Augusta</a></i> and other books from obscure authors. However, there remains a number of reliable accounts of Roman history.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Roman_times">In Roman times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ancient_Rome&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: In Roman times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first historians used their works for the lauding of Roman culture and customs. By the end of Republic, some historians distorted their histories to flatter their patrons—especially at the time of <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Marius</a>'s and <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>'s clash.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[269]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesar</a> wrote his own histories to make a complete account of his military campaigns in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> and during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_Civil_War" title="Caesar's Civil War">Civil War</a>.
</p><p>In the Empire, the biographies of famous men and early emperors flourished, examples being <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Caesars" title="The Twelve Caesars">The Twelve Caesars</a></i> of Suetonius, and Plutarch's <i><a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">Parallel Lives</a></i>. Other major works of Imperial times were that of Livy and Tacitus.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">The Histories</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sallust" title="Sallust">Sallust</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/Catiline" title="Catiline">Bellum Catilinae</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jugurthine_War" title="Jugurthine War">Bellum Jugurthinum</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> – <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Bello_Gallico" title="De Bello Gallico">De Bello Gallico</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/De_Bello_Civili" title="De Bello Civili">De Bello Civili</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Livy" title="Livy">Livy</a> – <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab_Urbe_Condita_Libri_(Livy)" title="Ab Urbe Condita Libri (Livy)">Ab urbe condita</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dionysius_of_Halicarnassus" title="Dionysius of Halicarnassus">Dionysius of Halicarnassus</a> – <i>Roman Antiquities</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> – <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naturalis_Historia" title="Naturalis Historia">Naturalis Historia</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/The_Jewish_War" title="The Jewish War">The Jewish War</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/The_Twelve_Caesars" title="The Twelve Caesars">The Twelve Caesars</a></i> (<i>De Vita Caesarum</i>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annales</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Tacitus)" title="Histories (Tacitus)">Histories</a></i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">Parallel Lives</a></i> (a series of biographies of famous Roman and Greek men)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cassius_Dio" title="Cassius Dio">Cassius Dio</a> – <i>Historia Romana</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Herodian" title="Herodian">Herodian</a> – <i>History of the Roman Empire since Marcus Aurelius</i></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ammianus_Marcellinus" title="Ammianus Marcellinus">Ammianus Marcellinus</a> – <i>Res Gestae</i></li></ul>

<p><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Italian_history" title="Timeline of Italian history">Timeline</a><br/>
</p>
<img alt="flag" class="noviewer thumbborder" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Flag_of_Italy.svg/16px-Flag_of_Italy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Flag_of_Italy.svg/24px-Flag_of_Italy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/03/Flag_of_Italy.svg/32px-Flag_of_Italy.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Italy" title="Portal:Italy">Italy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_Italy" title="Template:History of Italy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_Italy" title="Template talk:History of Italy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Italy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Interest in studying, and even idealizing, ancient Rome became prevalent during the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Renaissance" title="Italian Renaissance">Italian Renaissance</a>, and continues until the present day. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_de_Secondat,_baron_de_Montesquieu" title="Charles de Secondat, baron de Montesquieu">Charles Montesquieu</a> wrote a work <i>Reflections on the Causes of the Grandeur and Declension of the Romans</i>. The first major work was <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_the_Decline_and_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire">The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>, which encompassed the Roman civilization from the end of the 2nd century to the fall of the Byzantine Empire in 1453.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbon2_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbon2-273">[270]</a></sup> Like Montesquieu, Gibbon paid tribute to the virtue of Roman citizens. <a href="/wiki/Barthold_Georg_Niebuhr" title="Barthold Georg Niebuhr">Barthold Georg Niebuhr</a> was a founder of the examination of ancient Roman history and wrote <i>The Roman History</i>, tracing the period until the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic war</a>. Niebuhr tried to determine the way the Roman tradition evolved. According to him, Romans, like other people, had an historical <a href="/wiki/Ethos" title="Ethos">ethos</a> preserved mainly in the noble families.
</p><p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleonic</a> period a work titled <i>The History of Romans</i> by <a href="/wiki/Victor_Duruy" title="Victor Duruy">Victor Duruy</a> appeared. It highlighted the <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Caesarean</a> period popular at the time. <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Rome_(Mommsen)" title="History of Rome (Mommsen)">History of Rome</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_constitutional_law" title="Roman constitutional law">Roman constitutional law</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Inscriptionum_Latinarum" title="Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum">Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum</a></i>, all by <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Mommsen" title="Theodor Mommsen">Theodor Mommsen</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirjasto_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-kirjasto-274">[271]</a></sup> became very important milestones. Later the work <i>Greatness and Decline of Rome</i> by <a href="/wiki/Guglielmo_Ferrero" title="Guglielmo Ferrero">Guglielmo Ferrero</a> was published. The Russian work <i>Очерки по истории римского землевладения, преимущественно в эпоху Империи</i> (<i>The Outlines on Roman Landownership History, Mainly During the Empire</i>) by Ivan Grevs contained information on the economy of <a href="/wiki/Titus_Pomponius_Atticus" title="Titus Pomponius Atticus">Pomponius Atticus</a>, one of the largest landowners at the end of the Republic.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> – <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_the_Decline_and_Fall_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire">The History of the Decline and Fall of the Roman Empire</a></i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Bagnall_Bury" title="John Bagnall Bury">John Bagnall Bury</a> – <i>History of the Later Roman Empire</i></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michael_Grant_(author)" title="Michael Grant (author)">Michael Grant</a> – <i>The Roman World</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[272]</a></sup></li>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This splintering is a landmark historians use to divide the ancient period of <a href="/wiki/Universal_history" title="Universal history">universal history</a> from the pre-medieval "<a href="/wiki/Dark_Ages_(historiography)" title="Dark Ages (historiography)">Dark Ages</a>" of Europe.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the citizens of the empire made no distinction, the empire is most commonly referred to as the "Byzantine Empire" by modern historians to differentiate between the state in antiquity and the state during the Middle Ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Later in Christian liturgy, "noon" came to describe the <a href="/wiki/Nones_(liturgy)" title="Nones (liturgy)">nones</a>, a time of prayer originally at 3 pm but later at midday, so "noon" became synonymous with midday.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-NCBIAntonio2019-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-NCBIAntonio2019_256-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-NCBIAntonio2019_256-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFAntonioGaoMootsLucci2019">Antonio, Margaret L.; Gao, Ziyue; Moots, Hannah M.; Lucci, Michaela; Candilio, Francesca;  et al. <li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.2019Table_S2_Sample_Information-257"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.2019Table_S2_Sample_Information_257-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAntonio_et_al.2019">Antonio et al. 2019</a>, Table S2 Sample Information.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191-2_Fig._2-258"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20191-2_Fig._2_258-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAntonio_et_al.2019">Antonio et al. 2019</a>, pp. 1-2 Fig. 2.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-259"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-259">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAntonio_et_al.2019">Antonio et al. 2019</a>, p. 4. "[C]ompared to Iron Age individuals, the Imperial population shares more alleles with early Bronze Age Jordanians... Notably, only 2 out of 48 Imperial-era individuals fall in the European cluster (C7) to which 8 out of 11 Iron Age individuals belong... [F]ew Imperial individuals (n = 2) have strong genetic affinities to western Mediterranean populations."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Wade_673-260"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Wade_673_260-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wade_673_260-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Wade_673_260-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWade2019">Wade 2019</a>, p. 673. "People from the city's earliest eras and from after the Western empire's decline in the fourth century C.E. genetically resembled other Western Europeans. But during the imperial period most sampled residents had Eastern Mediterranean or Middle Eastern ancestry... The study suggests the vast majority of immigrants to Rome came from the East. Of 48 individuals sampled from this period, only two showed strong genetic ties to Europe... Invading barbarians brought in more European ancestry. Rome gradually lost its strong genetic link to the Eastern Mediterranean and Middle East. By medieval times, city residents again genetically resembled European populations."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20193–6-261"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAntonio_et_al.20193–6_261-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFAntonio_et_al.2019">Antonio et al. 2019</a>, pp. 3–6.</span>
</li>
